<a href="/wiki/Specials_(Unicode_block)#Replacement_character" title="Specials (Unicode block)">question marks, boxes, or other symbols</a> instead of <a href="/wiki/Chinese_characters" title="Chinese characters">Chinese characters</a>.</td>\n</tr>\n</table>\n<table role="presentation" class="metadata mbox-small noprint selfref" style="background-color:#f9f9f9;border:1px solid #aaa;color:#000;width: 22em;">\n<tr>\n<td style="width:1px"></td>\n<td class="mbox-text plainlist" style="vertical-align:middle; font-size:95%;"><b>This article contains <a href="/wiki/Help:Special_characters" title="Help:Special characters">special characters</a>.</b> Without proper <a href="/wiki/Help:Special_characters" title="Help:Special characters">rendering support</a>, you may see <a href="/wiki/Specials_(Unicode_block)#Replacement_character" title="Specials (Unicode block)">question marks, boxes, or other symbols</a>.</td>\n</tr>\n</table>\n<p><b>Cantonese</b>, or <b>Standard Cantonese</b>, is a <a href="/wiki/Varieties_of_Chinese" title="Varieties of Chinese">variety</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Chinese_language" title="Chinese language">Chinese language</a> spoken within <a href="/wiki/Guangzhou" title="Guangzhou">Guangzhou</a> (historically Canton) and its vicinity in southeastern China. It is the traditional <a href="/wiki/Prestige_(sociolinguistics)" title="Prestige (sociolinguistics)">prestige variety</a> of <a href="/wiki/Yue_Chinese" title="Yue Chinese">Yue</a>, one of the major subdivisions of Chinese.</p>\n<p>In <a href="/wiki/Mainland_China" title="Mainland China">mainland China</a>, it is the <i><a href="/wiki/Lingua_franca" title="Lingua franca">lingua franca</a></i> of the province of <a href="/wiki/Guangdong" title="Guangdong">Guangdong</a>, being the majority language of the <a href="/wiki/Pearl_River_Delta" title="Pearl River Delta">Pearl River Delta</a>, and neighbouring areas such as <a href="/wiki/Guangxi" title="Guangxi">Guangxi</a>. It is the dominant and official language of <a href="/wiki/Hong_Kong" title="Hong Kong">Hong Kong</a> and <a href="/wiki/Macau" title="Macau">Macau</a>. Cantonese is also widely spoken amongst <a href="/wiki/Overseas_Chinese" title="Overseas Chinese">overseas Chinese</a> in <a href="/wiki/Southeast_Asia" title="Southeast Asia">Southeast Asia</a> (most notably in <a href="/wiki/Vietnam" title="Vietnam">Vietnam</a> and <a href="/wiki/Malaysia" title="Malaysia">Malaysia</a>, as well as in <a href="/wiki/Singapore" title="Singapore">Singapore</a> and <a href="/wiki/Cambodia" title="Cambodia">Cambodia</a> to a lesser extent) and throughout the <a href="/wiki/Western_world" title="Western world">Western world</a>.</p>\n<p>While the term <i>Cantonese</i> refers narrowly to the <a href="/wiki/Prestige_variety" class="mw-redirect" title="Prestige variety">prestige variety</a>, it is often used in a broader sense for the entire Yue subdivision of Chinese, including related but largely <a href="/wiki/Mutually_unintelligible" class="mw-redirect" title="Mutually unintelligible">mutually unintelligible</a> languages such as <a href="/wiki/Taishanese" title="Taishanese">Taishanese</a>. When Cantonese and the closely related <a href="/wiki/Yuehai_dialects" title="Yuehai dialects">Yuehai dialects</a> are classified together, there are about 80 million total speakers.<sup id="cite_ref-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-3">[3]</a></sup> Cantonese is viewed as a vital part of the cultural identity for its native speakers across large swaths of <a href="/wiki/Liangguang" title="Liangguang">southeastern China</a>, Hong Kong and Macau.</p>\n<p>Although Cantonese shares some vocabulary with <a href="/wiki/Mandarin_language" class="mw-redirect" title="Mandarin language">Mandarin</a>, the two varieties are <a href="/wiki/Mutually_unintelligible" class="mw-redirect" title="Mutually unintelligible">mutually unintelligible</a> because of differences in pronunciation, grammar and lexicon. Sentence structure, in particular the placement of verbs, sometimes differs between the two varieties. A notable difference between Cantonese and Mandarin is how the spoken word is written; both can be recorded verbatim but very few Cantonese speakers are knowledgeable in the full Cantonese written vocabulary, so a non-verbatim formalised written form is adopted which is more akin to the Mandarin written form.<sup id="cite_ref-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-4">[4]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-5">[5]</a></sup> This results in the situation in which a Cantonese and a Mandarin text may look similar, but are pronounced differently.</p>\n<div class="toclimit-3">\n<div id="toc" class="toc">\n<div class="toctitle">\n<h2>Contents</h2>\n</div>\n<ul>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-1"><a href="#Names"><span role</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-22"><a href="#Phonology"><span class="tocnumber">5</span> <span class="toctext">Phonology</span></a>\n<ul>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-23"><a href="#Initials_and_finals"><span class="tocnumber">5.1</span> <span class="toctext">Initials and finals</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-24"><a href="#Tones"><span class="tocnumber">5.2</span> <span class="toctext">Tones</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-25"><a href="#Written_Cantonese"><span class="tocnumber">6</span> <span class="toctext">Written Cantonese</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-26"><a href="#Romanization"><span class="tocnumber">7</span> <span class="toctext">Romanization</span></a>\n<ul>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-27"><a href="#Early_Western_effort"><span class="tocnumber">7.1</span> <span class="toctext">Early Western effort</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-28"><a href="#Cantonese_romanization_in_Hong_Kong"><span class="tocnumber">7.2</span> <span class="toctext">Cantonese romanization in Hong Kong</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-29"><a href="#Comparison"><span class="tocnumber">7.3</span> <span class="toctext">Comparison</span></a>\n<ul>\n<li class="toclevel-3 tocsection-30"><a href="#Initials"><span class="tocnumber">7.3.1</span> <span class="toctext">Initials</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-3 tocsection-31"><a href="#Finals"><span class="tocnumber">7.3.2</span> <span class="toctext">Finals</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-3 tocsection-32"><a href="#Tones_2"><span class="tocnumber">7.3.3</span> <span class="toctext">Tones</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</li>\n</ul>\n</li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-33"><a href="#Loanwords"><span class="tocnumber">8</span> <span class="toctext">Loanwords</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-34"><a href="#See_also"><span class="tocnumber">9</span> <span class="toctext">See also</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-35"><a href="#Footnotes"><span class="tocnumber">10</span> <span class="toctext">Footnotes</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-36"><a href="#Further_reading"><span class="tocnumber">11</span> <span class="toctext">Further reading</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-37"><a href="#External_links"><span class="tocnumber">12</span> <span class="toctext">External links</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</div>\n</div>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Names">Names</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Cantonese&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Names">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<p>In English, the term "Cantonese" is ambiguous. Cantonese proper is the variety native to the city of Canton, which is the traditional English name of <a href="/wiki/Guangzhou" title="Guangzhou">Guangzhou</a>. This narrow sense may be specified as "Canton language" or "Guangzhou language" in English.<sup id="cite_ref-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-6">[6]</a></sup></p>\n<p>However, "Cantonese" may also refer to the primary branch of Cantonese that contains Cantonese proper as well as <a href="/wiki/Taishanese" title="Taishanese">Taishanese</a> and <a href="/wiki/Gao-Yang_Yue" title="Gao-Yang Yue">Gaoyang</a>; this broader usage may be specified as "Yue" (<sup><a href="/wiki/Simplified_Chinese_characters" title="Simplified Chinese characters">s</a></sup><span lang="zh-s">\xe7\xb2\xa4</span>; <sup><a href="/wiki/Traditional_Chinese_characters" title="Traditional Chinese characters">t</a></sup><span lang="zh-t">\xe7\xb2\xb5</span>). In this article, "Cantonese" is used for Cantonese proper.</p>\n<p>Historically, speakers called this variety <b>"Canton speech"</b> or <b>"Guangzhou speech"</b> (<span lang="zh-Hans">\xe5\xb9\xbf\xe5\xb7\x9e\xe8\xaf\x9d</span>; <span lang="zh-Hant">\xe5\xbb\xa3\xe5\xb7\x9e\xe8\xa9\xb1</span>; <i><span lang="yue">Gw\xc3\xb3ngj\xc4\x81u w\xc3\xa1</span></i>), although this term is now seldom used outside mainland China. In Guangdong province, people also call it <b>"provincial capital speech"</b> (<span lang="zh-Hans">\xe7\x9c\x81\xe5\x9f\x8e\xe8\xaf\x9d</span>; <span lang="zh-Hant">\xe7\x9c\x81\xe5\x9f\x8e\xe8\xa9\xb1</span>; <i><span lang="yue">S\xc3\xa1angs\xc3\xacng w\xc3\xa1</span></i>) or <b>"plain speech"</b> (<span lang="zh-Hans">\xe7\x99\xbd\xe8\xaf\x9d</span>; <span lang="zh-Hant">\xe7\x99\xbd\xe8\xa9\xb1</span>; <i><span lang="yue">Baahk w\xc3\xa1</span></i>)</p>\n<p>In Hong Kong and Macau, as well as among overseas Chinese communities, the language is referred to as <b>"Guangdong speech"</b> (<span lang="zh-Hans">\xe5\xb9\xbf\xe4\xb8\x9c\xe8\xaf\x9d</span>; <span lang="zh-Hant">\xe5\xbb\xa3\xe6\x9d\xb1\xe8\xa9\xb1</span>; <i><span lang="yue">Gw\xc3\xb3ngd\xc5\xabng w\xc3\xa1</span></i>) or simply "Chinese" (<a href="/wiki/Chinese_language" title="Chinese language">Chinese</a>: <span lang="zh">\xe4\xb8\xad\xe6\x96\x87</span>; <a href="/wiki/Yale_romanization_of_Cantonese" title="Yale romanization of Cantonese">Cantonese Yale</a>: <i><span lang="yue">J\xc5\xabngm\xc3\xa1n</span></i>).<sup id="cite_ref-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-7">[7]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-8">[8]</a></sup> In mainland China, the term <b>"Guangdong speech"</b> is also increasingly being used among both native and non-native speakers. Given the history of the development of Yue dialects during the <a href="/wiki/Tang_dynasty" title="Tang dynasty">Tang dynasty</a> migrations to the region, in overseas Chinese communities, it is also referred to as \xe5\x94\x90\xe8\xa9\xb1, given that people refer to themselves as \xe5\x94\x90\xe4\xba\xba.</p>\n<p>Due to its status as a <a href="/wiki/Prestige_dialect" class="mw-redirect" title="Prestige dialect">prestige dialect</a> among all the dialects of the Yue branch of Chinese varieties, it is often called "Standard Cantonese" (<span lang="zh-Hans">\xe6\xa0\x87\xe5\x87\x86\xe7\xb2\xa4\xe8\xaf\xad</span>; <span lang="zh-Hant">\xe6\xa8\x99\xe6\xba\x96\xe7\xb2\xb5\xe8\xaa\x9e</span>; <i><span lang="yue">B\xc4\xabuj\xc3\xa9un Yuhty\xc3\xbah</span></i>).</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Geographic_distribution">Geographic distribution</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Cantonese&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Geographic distribution">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Hong_Kong_and_Macau">Hong Kong and Macau</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Cantonese&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Hong Kong and Macau">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/Hong_Kong_Cantonese" title="Hong Kong Cantonese">Hong Kong Cantonese</a></div>\n<p>The <a href="/wiki/Official_language" title="Official language">official languages</a> of <a href="/wiki/Hong_Kong" title="Hong Kong">Hong Kong</a> are <a href="/wiki/Chinese_language" title="Chinese language">Chinese</a> and <a href="/wiki/English_language" title="English language">English</a>, as defined in the <a href="/wiki/Hong_Kong_Basic_Law" title="Hong Kong Basic Law">Hong Kong Basic Law</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-9">[9]</a></sup> The Chinese language has <a href="/wiki/Varieties_of_Chinese" title="Varieties of Chinese">many different varieties</a>, of which Cantonese is one. Given the traditional predominance of Cantonese within Hong Kong, it is the <i>de facto</i> official spoken form of the Chinese language used in the <a href="/wiki/Government_of_Hong_Kong" title="Government of Hong Kong">Hong Kong Government</a> and all courts and tribunals. It is also used as the <a href="/wiki/Medium_of_instruction" title="Medium of instruction">medium of instruction</a> in schools, alongside English.</p>\n<p>A similar situation also exists in neighboring <a href="/wiki/Macau" title="Macau">Macau</a>, where Chinese is an official language along with <a href="/wiki/Portuguese_language" title="Portuguese language">Portuguese</a>. As in Hong Kong, Cantonese is the predominant spoken <a href="/wiki/Varieties_of_Chinese" title="Varieties of Chinese">variety of Chinese</a> used in everyday life and is thus the official form of Chinese used in the government. The Cantonese spoken in Hong Kong and Macau is <a href="/wiki/Mutually_intelligible" class="mw-redirect" title="Mutually intelligible">mutually intelligible</a> with the Cantonese spoken in the mainland city of Guangzhou, although there exist some minor differences in accent, pronunciation and vocabulary.</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="China">China</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Cantonese&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: China">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tright">\n<div class="thumbinner" style="width:272px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Ping_and_Yue_dialect_map.svg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/14/Ping_and_Yue_dialect_map.svg/270px-Ping_and_Yue_dialect_map.svg.png" width="270" height="170" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/14/Ping_and_Yue_dialect_map.svg/405px-Ping_and_Yue_dialect_map.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/14/Ping_and_Yue_dialect_map.svg/540px-Ping_and_Yue_dialect_map.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="512" data-file-height="323" /></a>\n<div class="thumbcaption">\n<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Ping_and_Yue_dialect_map.svg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>\nDistribution of Yue Chinese languages in Southeastern China. Standard Cantonese and closely related dialects are highlighted in pink.</div>\n</div>\n</div>\n<p>Cantonese first developed around the port city of <a href="/wiki/Guangzhou" title="Guangzhou">Guangzhou</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Pearl_River_Delta" title="Pearl River Delta">Pearl River Delta</a> region of southeastern <a href="/wiki/China" title="China">China</a>. Due to the city\'s long standing as an important cultural center, Cantonese emerged as the prestige dialect of the <a href="/wiki/Yue_Chinese" title="Yue Chinese">Yue varieties of Chinese</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Southern_Song" class="mw-redirect" title="Southern Song">Southern Song</a> dynasty and its usage spread around most of what is now the provinces of <a href="/wiki/Guangdong" title="Guangdong">Guangdong</a> and <a href="/wiki/Guangxi" title="Guangxi">Guangxi</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEYue-Hashimoto19724_10-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEYue-Hashimoto19724-10">[10]</a></sup></p>\n<p>Despite the cession of Macau to Portugal in 1557 and <a href="/wiki/Treaty_of_Nanking" title="Treaty of Nanking">Hong Kong to Britain</a> in 1842, the ethnic Chinese population of the two territories largely originated from the 19th and 20th century immigration from Guangzhou and surrounding areas, making Cantonese the prominent Chinese language in the territories. On the mainland, Cantonese continued to serve as the <i><a href="/wiki/Lingua_franca" title="Lingua franca">lingua franca</a></i> of <a href="/wiki/Guangdong" title="Guangdong">Guangdong</a> and <a href="/wiki/Guangxi" title="Guangxi">Guangxi</a> provinces even after Mandarin was made the official language of the government by the <a href="/wiki/Qing_Dynasty" class="mw-redirect" title="Qing Dynasty">Qing Dynasty</a> in the early 1900s.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECoblin2000549.E2.80.93550_11-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECoblin2000549.E2.80.93550-11">[11]</a></sup> Cantonese remained the dominant and influential language in southeastern China until the establishment of the <a href="/wiki/People%27s_Republic_of_China" class="mw-redirect" title="People\'s Republic of China">People\'s Republic of China</a> in 1949 and its promotion of <a href="/wiki/Standard_Chinese" title="Standard Chinese">Standard Chinese</a> as the sole official language of the nation throughout the last half of the 20th century, although its influence is still strong in the region.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERamsey19873.E2.80.9315_12-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERamsey19873.E2.80.9315-12">[12]</a></sup></p>\n<p>While the Chinese government discourages the use of all forms of Chinese except Standard Chinese, Cantonese enjoys a relatively higher standing than other Chinese languages, with its own media and usage in public transportation in Guangdong province.<sup id="cite_ref-sarft-1_13-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-sarft-1-13">[13]</a></sup> Furthermore, it is also a medium of instruction in select academic curricula, including some university elective courses and <a href="/wiki/Chinese_as_a_foreign_language" title="Chinese as a foreign language">Chinese as a foreign language</a> programs.<sup id="cite_ref-14" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-14">[14]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-15" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-15">[15]</a></sup> The permitted usage of Cantonese in mainland China is largely a countermeasure against Hong Kong\'s influence, as the autonomous territory has the right to freedom of the press and speech and its Cantonese-language media have a substantial exposure and following in Guangdong.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEZhangYang2004154_16-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEZhangYang2004154-16">[16]</a></sup></p>\n<p>Nevertheless, the place of local Cantonese language and culture remains contentious. A 2010 <a href="/wiki/Guangzhou_Television_Cantonese_controversy" title="Guangzhou Television Cantonese controversy">proposal to switch some programming on Guangzhou television</a> from Cantonese to Mandarin was abandoned following massive public protests, the largest since the <a href="/wiki/Tiananmen_Square_protests_of_1989" title="Tiananmen Square protests of 1989">Tiananmen Square protests of 1989</a>. As a major economic center of China, there have been recent concerns that the use of Cantonese in Guangzhou is diminishing in favour of Mandarin, both through the continual influx of Mandarin-speaking migrants from poorer areas and strict government policies. As a result, Cantonese is being given a more important status by the natives than ever before as a common identity of the local people.<sup id="cite_ref-17" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-17">[17]</a></sup></p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Southeast_Asia">Southeast Asia</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Cantonese&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Southeast Asia">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>Cantonese has historically served as a <i>lingua franca</i> among overseas Chinese in Southeast Asia, who speak a variety of other forms of Chinese including <a href="/wiki/Hokkien" title="Hokkien">Hokkien</a>, <a href="/wiki/Teochew_dialect" title="Teochew dialect">Teochew</a> and <a href="/wiki/Hakka_Chinese" title="Hakka Chinese">Hakka</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-google1_18-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-google1-18">[18]</a></sup> Additionally, Cantonese media and pop culture from Hong Kong is popular throughout the region.</p>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Vietnam">Vietnam</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Cantonese&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Vietnam">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/Hoa_people" title="Hoa people">Hoa people</a></div>\n<p>In <a href="/wiki/Vietnam" title="Vietnam">Vietnam</a>, Cantonese is the dominant language of the ethnic Chinese community, usually referred to as <i><a href="/wiki/Hoa_people" title="Hoa people">Hoa</a></i>, which numbers about one million people and constitutes one of the largest minority groups in the country.<sup id="cite_ref-GSO2009_19-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-GSO2009-19">[19]</a></sup> Over half of the ethnic Chinese population in Vietnam speaks Cantonese as a native language and the variety also serves as a lingua franca between the different Chinese dialect groups. Many speakers reflect their exposure to <a href="/wiki/Vietnamese_language" title="Vietnamese language">Vietnamese</a> with a Vietnamese accent or a tendency to <a href="/wiki/Code-switching" title="Code-switching">code-switch</a> between Cantonese and Vietnamese.<sup id="cite_ref-20" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-20">[20]</a></sup></p>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Malaysia">Malaysia</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Cantonese&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Malaysia">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/Malaysian_Chinese#Cantonese" title="Malaysian Chinese">Malaysian Chinese \xc2\xa7&#160;Cantonese</a></div>\n<p>In <a href="/wiki/Malaysia" title="Malaysia">Malaysia</a>, Cantonese is widely spoken amongst the <a href="/wiki/Malaysian_Chinese" title="Malaysian Chinese">Malaysian Chinese</a> community in the capital city of <a href="/wiki/Kuala_Lumpur" title="Kuala Lumpur">Kuala Lumpur</a><sup id="cite_ref-21" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-21">[21]</a></sup> and the surrounding areas in the <a href="/wiki/Klang_Valley" title="Klang Valley">Klang Valley</a> (<a href="/wiki/Petaling_Jaya" title="Petaling Jaya">Petaling Jaya</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ampang,_Selangor" title="Ampang, Selangor">Ampang</a>, <a href="/wiki/Cheras,_Selangor" class="mw-redirect" title="Cheras, Selangor">Cheras</a>, <a href="/wiki/Shah_Alam" title="Shah Alam">Shah Alam</a>, <a href="/wiki/Kajang" title="Kajang">Kajang</a>, <a href="/wiki/Bangi,_Malaysia" title="Bangi, Malaysia">Bangi</a>, <a href="/wiki/Selayang" title="Selayang">Selayang</a> and <a href="/wiki/Subang_Jaya" title="Subang Jaya">Subang Jaya</a> with a small minority in the town of <a href="/wiki/Klang_(city)" title="Klang (city)">Klang</a>). The dialect is also widely spoken in the state of <a href="/wiki/Perak" title="Perak">Perak</a>, especially in the state capital city of <a href="/wiki/Ipoh" title="Ipoh">Ipoh</a>, as well as in the eastern <a href="/wiki/Sabah" title="Sabah">Sabahan</a> town of <a href="/wiki/Sandakan" title="Sandakan">Sandakan</a>. Although <a href="/wiki/Hokkien" title="Hokkien">Hokkien</a> is the most spoken variety of Chinese and Mandarin is the medium of education at <a href="/wiki/Chinese-language_schools" class="mw-redirect" title="Chinese-language schools">Chinese-language schools</a>, Cantonese is largely influential in the local Chinese-language media and is used in commerce by Chinese Malaysians.<sup id="cite_ref-22" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-22">[22]</a></sup></p>\n<p>Due to the popularity of <a href="/wiki/Hong_Kong_popular_culture" class="mw-redirect" title="Hong Kong popular culture">Hong Kong popular culture</a>, especially through drama series and popular music, Cantonese is widely understood by the Chinese in all parts of Malaysia, even though a large proportion of the Chinese Malaysian population is non-Cantonese. Television networks in Malaysia regularly broadcast Hong Kong television programmes in their original Cantonese audio and soundtrack. Cantonese radio is also available in the nation and Cantonese is prevalent in locally produced Chinese television.<sup id="cite_ref-23" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-23">[23]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-24" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-24">[24]</a></sup></p>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Singapore">Singapore</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Cantonese&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Singapore">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/Chinese_Singaporeans" title="Chinese Singaporeans">Chinese Singaporeans</a> and <a href="/wiki/Languages_of_Singapore" title="Languages of Singapore">Languages of Singapore</a></div>\n<p>In <a href="/wiki/Singapore" title="Singapore">Singapore</a>, Mandarin is the official variety of the Chinese language used by the government, which has a <a href="/wiki/Speak_Mandarin_Campaign" title="Speak Mandarin Campaign">Speak Mandarin Campaign</a> (SMC) seeking to actively promote the use of Mandarin over other Chinese varieties. Cantonese is spoken by a little over 15% of Chinese households in Singapore. Despite the government\'s active promotion of SMC, the Cantonese-speaking Chinese community has had relative success in preserving its language against Mandarin compared to other dialect groups.<sup id="cite_ref-25" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-25">[25]</a></sup></p>\n<p>Notably, all nationally produced non-Mandarin Chinese TV and radio programs were stopped after 1979.<sup id="cite_ref-cna_26-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-cna-26">[26]</a></sup> The prime minister, <a href="/wiki/Lee_Kuan_Yew" title="Lee Kuan Yew">Lee Kuan Yew</a>, then, also stopped giving speeches in <a href="/wiki/Hokkien" title="Hokkien">Hokkien</a> to prevent giving conflicting signals to the people.<sup id="cite_ref-cna_26-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-cna-26">[26]</a></sup> Hong Kong (Cantonese) and <a href="/wiki/Taiwanese_Hokkien" title="Taiwanese Hokkien">Taiwanese</a> dramas are unavailable in their untranslated form on free-to-air television, though drama series in non-Chinese languages are available in their original languages. Cantonese drama series on terrestrial TV channels are instead dubbed in Mandarin and broadcast without the original Cantonese audio and soundtrack. However, originals may be available through other sources such as cable television and online videos.</p>\n<p>Furthermore, an offshoot of SMC is the translation to <a href="/wiki/Hanyu_Pinyin" class="mw-redirect" title="Hanyu Pinyin">Hanyu Pinyin</a> of certain terms which originated from southern Chinese varieties. For instance, <a href="/wiki/Dim_sum" title="Dim sum">dim sum</a> is often known as <i>di\xc7\x8en x\xc4\xabn</i> in Singapore\'s English-language media, though this is largely a matter of style, and most Singaporeans will refer to it as <i>dim sum</i> when speaking English.<sup id="cite_ref-27" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-27">[27]</a></sup></p>\n<p>Nevertheless, since the government restriction on media in non-Mandarin varieties was relaxed in the mid-1990s and 2000s, the presence of Cantonese in Singapore has grown substantially. Forms of popular culture from Hong Kong, such as <a href="/wiki/Hong_Kong_television_drama" title="Hong Kong television drama">television series</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hong_Kong_cinema" class="mw-redirect" title="Hong Kong cinema">cinema</a> and <a href="/wiki/Cantopop" title="Cantopop">pop music</a> have become popular in Singaporean society, and non-dubbed original versions of the media became widely available. Consequently, there has been a large of number of non-Cantonese Chinese Singaporeans being able to understand or speak Cantonese to some varying extent, with a number of educational institutes offering Cantonese as an elective language course.<sup id="cite_ref-28" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-28">[28]</a></sup></p>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Cambodia">Cambodia</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Cantonese&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Cambodia">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>\n<p>Cantonese is widely used as the inter-communal language among <a href="/wiki/Chinese_Cambodian" title="Chinese Cambodian">Chinese Cambodians</a>, especially in <a href="/wiki/Phnom_Penh" title="Phnom Penh">Phnom Penh</a> and other urban areas. While Teochew speakers form the majority of the Chinese population in <a href="/wiki/Cambodia" title="Cambodia">Cambodia</a>, Cantonese is often used as a vernacular in commerce and with other Chinese variant groups in the nation.<sup id="cite_ref-29" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-29">[29]</a></sup> Chinese-language schools in Cambodia are conducted in both, Cantonese and Mandarin, but it always depends on the school.<sup id="cite_ref-30" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-30">[30]</a></sup></p>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Thailand">Thailand</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Cantonese&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Thailand">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>\n<p><a href="/wiki/Thailand" title="Thailand">Thailand</a> is home to the <a href="/wiki/Thai_Chinese" title="Thai Chinese">largest overseas Chinese community</a> in the world, numbering over 9 million individuals. Cantonese is the fourth most-spoken variety of Chinese in Thai Chinese households after <a href="/wiki/Teochew_language" class="mw-redirect" title="Teochew language">Teochew</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hakka_Chinese" title="Hakka Chinese">Hakka</a> and <a href="/wiki/Hainanese" title="Hainanese">Hainanese</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-31" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-31">[31]</a></sup> However, within the Thai Chinese commercial sector, it serves as a common language alongside Teochew or Thai. Chinese-language schools in Thailand have also traditionally been conducted in Cantonese. Furthermore, Cantonese serves as the lingua franca with other Chinese communities in the nation.<sup id="cite_ref-32" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-32">[32]</a></sup></p>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Indonesia">Indonesia</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Cantonese&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Indonesia">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/Indonesian_Chinese#Language" class="mw-redirect" title="Indonesian Chinese">Indonesian Chinese \xc2\xa7&#160;Language</a></div>\n<p>In <a href="/wiki/Indonesia" title="Indonesia">Indonesia</a>, Cantonese is locally known as <i>Konghu</i> and is one of the variants spoken by the <a href="/wiki/Chinese_Indonesian" class="mw-redirect" title="Chinese Indonesian">Chinese Indonesian</a> community, with speakers largely concentrated in major cities such as <a href="/wiki/Jakarta" title="Jakarta">Jakarta</a> (the capital city), <a href="/wiki/Medan" title="Medan">Medan</a> and <a href="/wiki/Batam" title="Batam">Batam</a>. However, it has a relatively minor presence compared to other Southeast Asian nations, being the fourth most spoken Chinese variety after Hokkien, Hakka and Teochew. <sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELewis2005391_33-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELewis2005391-33">[33]</a></sup></p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="North_America">North America</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Cantonese&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: North America">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="United_States">United States</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Cantonese&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: United States">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>\n<div class="thumb tright">\n<div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;"><a href="/wiki/File:San_Francisco_China_Town_MC.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/75/San_Francisco_China_Town_MC.jpg/250px-San_Francisco_China_Town_MC.jpg" width="250" height="167" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/75/San_Francisco_China_Town_MC.jpg/375px-San_Francisco_China_Town_MC.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/75/San_Francisco_China_Town_MC.jpg/500px-San_Francisco_China_Town_MC.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2250" data-file-height="1500" /></a>\n<div class="thumbcaption">\n<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:San_Francisco_China_Town_MC.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>\nStreet in <a href="/wiki/Chinatown,_San_Francisco" title="Chinatown, San Francisco">Chinatown, San Francisco</a>. Cantonese has traditionally been the dominant Chinese variant among Chinese populations in the Western world.</div>\n</div>\n</div>\n<p>Over a period of 150 years, Guangdong has been the place-of-origin for most Chinese emigrants to Western nations; one coastal county, <a href="/wiki/Taishan" title="Taishan">Taishan</a> (or T\xc3\xb3is\xc3\xa0n, where the S\xc3\xacy\xc3\xac or <i>sei yap</i> variety of Yue is spoken), alone may be the origin of the vast majority of Chinese immigrants to the U.S. before 1965.<sup id="cite_ref-34" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-34">[34]</a></sup> As a result, Yue languages such as Cantonese and the closely related variety of <a href="/wiki/Taishanese" title="Taishanese">Taishanese</a> have been the major Chinese varieties traditionally spoken in the United States.</p>\n<p>The Zhongshan variant of Cantonese, with origins in the western Pearl River Delta, is spoken by many Chinese immigrants in <a href="/wiki/Hawaii" title="Hawaii">Hawaii</a>, and some in <a href="/wiki/San_Francisco" title="San Francisco">San Francisco</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Sacramento_River" title="Sacramento River">Sacramento River</a> Delta (see <a href="/wiki/Locke,_California" title="Locke, California">Locke, California</a>); it is a Yuehai variety much like Guangzhou Cantonese, but has "flatter" tones. Chinese is the third most widely spoken non-English language in the United States when both Cantonese and Mandarin are combined, behind <a href="/wiki/Spanish_language" title="Spanish language">Spanish</a> and <a href="/wiki/French_language" title="French language">French</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Lai_35-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Lai-35">[35]</a></sup> Many institutes of higher education have traditionally had Chinese programs based on Cantonese, with some continuing to offer these programs despite the rise of Mandarin. The most popular romanization for learning Cantonese in the United States is <a href="/wiki/Yale_romanization_of_Cantonese" title="Yale romanization of Cantonese">Yale Romanization</a>.</p>\n<p>The majority of Chinese emigrants have traditionally originated from Guangdong and Guangxi, as well as Hong Kong and Macao (beginning in the latter half of the 20th century and before the <a href="/wiki/Handover_of_Hong_Kong" class="mw-redirect" title="Handover of Hong Kong">Handover</a>) and Southeast Asia, with Cantonese as their native language. However, more recent immigrants are arriving from the rest of mainland China and Taiwan and most often speak <a href="/wiki/Standard_Chinese" title="Standard Chinese">Standard Mandarin</a> (Putonghua) as their native language,<sup id="cite_ref-36" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-36">[36]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-37" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-37">[37]</a></sup> although some may also speak their native local variety, such as <a href="/wiki/Shanghainese" title="Shanghainese">Shanghainese</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hokkien" title="Hokkien">Hokkien</a>, <a href="/wiki/Fuzhounese" class="mw-redirect" title="Fuzhounese">Fuzhounese</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hakka_language" class="mw-redirect" title="Hakka language">Hakka</a>, etc. As a result, Mandarin is becoming more common among the <a href="/wiki/Chinese_American" class="mw-redirect" title="Chinese American">Chinese American</a> community.</p>\n<p>The increase of Mandarin-speaking communities has resulted in the rise of separate neighborhoods or enclaves segregated by the primary Chinese variety spoken. Socioeconomic statuses are also a factor as well.<sup id="cite_ref-Chee_Beng_Tan_2007_115_38-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Chee_Beng_Tan_2007_115-38">[38]</a></sup> For example, in <a href="/wiki/New_York_City" title="New York City">New York City</a>, Cantonese still predominates in the city\'s <a href="/wiki/Mott_Street" title="Mott Street">older, traditional western portion</a> of <a href="/wiki/Chinatown,_Manhattan" title="Chinatown, Manhattan">Chinatown</a> in <a href="/wiki/Manhattan" title="Manhattan">Manhattan</a>, in Brooklyn\'s small new Chinatowns in sections of <a href="/wiki/Bensonhurst,_Brooklyn" title="Bensonhurst, Brooklyn">Bensonhurst</a> and in <a href="/wiki/Homecrest,_Brooklyn" title="Homecrest, Brooklyn">Homecrest</a>. The newly emerged <a href="/wiki/Little_Fuzhou" title="Little Fuzhou">Little Fuzhou</a> eastern portion of Manhattan\'s Chinatown and <a href="/wiki/Chinatown,_Brooklyn" class="mw-redirect" title="Chinatown, Brooklyn">Brooklyn\'s main large Chinatown</a> in and around <a href="/wiki/Sunset_Park,_Brooklyn" title="Sunset Park, Brooklyn">Sunset Park</a> are mostly populated by <a href="/wiki/Fuzhounese" class="mw-redirect" title="Fuzhounese">Fuzhounese speakers</a>, who often speak Mandarin as well. The Cantonese and Fuzhounese enclaves in New York City are more working class. <a href="/wiki/Chinatown,_Flushing" class="mw-redirect" title="Chinatown, Flushing">Flushing\'s large Chinatown</a>, which now holds the crown as the largest Chinatown of the city and <a href="/wiki/Elmhurst,_Queens" title="Elmhurst, Queens">Elmhurst\'s smaller Chinatown</a> in Queens are very mixed with large numbers of Mandarin speakers from many different parts of China and Taiwan. They comprise the primary cultural center for New York City\'s Chinese population and are more middle class.<sup id="cite_ref-Semplep2_39-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Semplep2-39">[39]</a></sup></p>\n<p>In <a href="/wiki/Northern_California" title="Northern California">Northern California</a>, especially in the <a href="/wiki/San_Francisco_Bay_Area" title="San Francisco Bay Area">San Francisco Bay Area</a>, Cantonese has historically and continues to predominate in the Chinatowns of <a href="/wiki/Chinatown,_San_Francisco" title="Chinatown, San Francisco">San Francisco</a> and <a href="/wiki/Chinatown,_Oakland" title="Chinatown, Oakland">Oakland</a>, as well as the surrounding suburbs and metropolitan area, although Mandarin is now<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Manual_of_Style/Dates_and_numbers#Chronological_items" title="Wikipedia:Manual of Style/Dates and numbers"><span title="The time period mentioned near this tag is ambiguous. (September 2016)">when?</span></a></i>]</sup> also found in <a href="/wiki/Silicon_Valley" title="Silicon Valley">Silicon Valley</a>. In contrast, <a href="/wiki/Southern_California" title="Southern California">Southern California</a> hosts a much larger Mandarin-speaking population, with Cantonese found in more historical Chinese communities such as that of <a href="/wiki/Chinatown,_Los_Angeles" title="Chinatown, Los Angeles">Chinatown, Los Angeles</a> and older Chinese ethnoburbs such as <a href="/wiki/San_Gabriel,_California" title="San Gabriel, California">San Gabriel</a>, <a href="/wiki/Rosemead,_California" title="Rosemead, California">Rosemead</a> and <a href="/wiki/Temple_City,_California" title="Temple City, California">Temple City</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-dragon_40-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-dragon-40">[40]</a></sup></p>\n<p>While a number of more-established Taiwanese immigrants have learned Cantonese to foster relations with the traditional Cantonese-speaking Chinese American population, more recent arrivals and the larger number of mainland Chinese immigrants have largely continued to use Mandarin as the exclusive variety of Chinese. This has led to a <a href="/wiki/Linguistic_discrimination" title="Linguistic discrimination">linguistic discrimination</a> that has also contributed to social conflicts between the two sides, with a growing number of Chinese Americans (including <a href="/wiki/American-born_Chinese" title="American-born Chinese">American-born Chinese</a>) of Cantonese background defending the historic Chinese American culture against the impacts of increasing Mandarin-speaking new arrivals.<sup id="cite_ref-Chee_Beng_Tan_2007_115_38-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Chee_Beng_Tan_2007_115-38">[38]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-HML_41-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-HML-41">[41]</a></sup></p>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Canada">Canada</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Cantonese&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Canada">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>\n<p>Cantonese is the second most common Chinese variety spoken among <a href="/wiki/Chinese_Canadians" title="Chinese Canadians">Chinese Canadians</a>. According to the <a href="/wiki/Canada_2016_Census" title="Canada 2016 Census">Canada 2016 Census</a>, there were 565,275 Canadian residents who reported Cantonese as their native language.</p>\n<p>As in the United States, the Chinese Canadian community traces its roots to early immigrants from Guangdong during the latter half of the 19th century.<sup id="cite_ref-42" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-42">[42]</a></sup> Later Chinese immigrants came from Hong Kong in two waves, first in the late 1960s to mid 1970s, and again in the 1980s to late 1990s on fears arising from the impending handover to the <a href="/wiki/People%27s_Republic_of_China" class="mw-redirect" title="People\'s Republic of China">People\'s Republic of China</a>. Chinese-speaking immigrants from conflict zones in Southeast Asia, especially Vietnam, arrived as well, beginning in the mid-1970s and were also largely Cantonese-speaking. Unlike the United States, recent immigration from mainland China and Taiwan to Canada has been small, and Cantonese still remains the predominant Chinese variety in the country.<sup id="cite_ref-autogenerated4_43-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated4-43">[43]</a></sup></p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Western_Europe">Western Europe</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Cantonese&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Western Europe">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="United_Kingdom">United Kingdom</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Cantonese&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: United Kingdom">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>\n<p>The overwhelming majority of Chinese speakers in the <a href="/wiki/United_Kingdom" title="United Kingdom">United Kingdom</a> use Cantonese, with about 300,000 British people claiming it as their first language.<sup id="cite_ref-44" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-44">[44]</a></sup> This is largely due to the presence of <a href="/wiki/Hong_Kong_people_in_the_United_Kingdom" title="Hong Kong people in the United Kingdom">British Hong Kongers</a> and the fact that many <a href="/wiki/British_Chinese" title="British Chinese">British Chinese</a> also have origins in the former British colonies in Southeast Asia of Singapore and Malaysia.</p>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="France">France</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Cantonese&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: France">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>\n<p>Among the <a href="/wiki/Chinese_community_in_France" class="mw-redirect" title="Chinese community in France">Chinese community in France</a>, Cantonese is spoken by immigrants who fled the former <a href="/wiki/French_Indochina" title="French Indochina">French Indochina</a> (Vietnam, Cambodia and Laos) following the conflicts and communist takeovers in the region during the 1970s. While a slight majority of ethnic Chinese from Indochina speak Teochew at home, knowledge of Cantonese is prevalent due to its historic prestige status in the region and is used for commercial and community purposes between the different Chinese variety groups. As in the United States, there is a divide between Cantonese-speakers and those speaking other mainland Chinese varieties.<sup id="cite_ref-45" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-45">[45]</a></sup></p>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Portugal">Portugal</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Cantonese&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Portugal">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>\n<p>Cantonese is spoken by ethnic <a href="/wiki/Chinese_people_in_Portugal" title="Chinese people in Portugal">Chinese in Portugal</a> who originate from Macau, the most established Chinese community in the nation with a presence dating back to the 16th century and <a href="/wiki/Portuguese_Macau" title="Portuguese Macau">Portuguese colonialism</a>. Since the late-20th century however, Mandarin and Wu-speaking migrants from mainland China have outnumbered those from Macau, although Cantonese is still retained among mainstream Chinese community associations.<sup id="cite_ref-46" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-46">[46]</a></sup></p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Australia">Australia</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Cantonese&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Australia">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>Cantonese has traditionally been the dominant Chinese language of the <a href="/wiki/Chinese_Australian" class="mw-redirect" title="Chinese Australian">Chinese Australian</a> community since the first ethnic Chinese settlers arrived in the 1850s. It maintained this status until the mid-2000s, when a heavy increase in immigration from Mandarin-speakers largely from Mainland China led to Mandarin surpassing Cantonese as the dominant Chinese dialect spoken. Cantonese is the third most spoken language in Australia. In the 2011 census, the Australian Bureau of Statistics listed 336,410 and 263,673 speakers of Mandarin and Cantonese, respectively.</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Cantonese&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: History">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/Yue_Chinese" title="Yue Chinese">Yue Chinese</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tright">\n<div class="thumbinner" style="width:142px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Tangyun_-_Chinese_Dictionary_Museum.JPG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d1/Tangyun_-_Chinese_Dictionary_Museum.JPG/140px-Tangyun_-_Chinese_Dictionary_Museum.JPG" width="140" height="201" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d1/Tangyun_-_Chinese_Dictionary_Museum.JPG/210px-Tangyun_-_Chinese_Dictionary_Museum.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d1/Tangyun_-_Chinese_Dictionary_Museum.JPG/280px-Tangyun_-_Chinese_Dictionary_Museum.JPG 2x" data-file-width="1564" data-file-height="2240" /></a>\n<div class="thumbcaption">\n<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Tangyun_-_Chinese_Dictionary_Museum.JPG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>\nChinese dictionary from the <a href="/wiki/Tang_dynasty" title="Tang dynasty">Tang dynasty</a>. Modern Cantonese pronunciation preserves almost all terminal consonants (-m -n -ng, -p -t -k) from Middle Chinese.</div>\n</div>\n</div>\n<p>During the <a href="/wiki/Southern_Song" class="mw-redirect" title="Southern Song">Southern Song</a> period, Guangzhou became the cultural center of the region.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEYue-Hashimoto19724_10-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEYue-Hashimoto19724-10">[10]</a></sup> Cantonese emerged as the <a href="/wiki/Prestige_(sociolinguistics)" title="Prestige (sociolinguistics)">prestige variety</a> of <a href="/wiki/Yue_Chinese" title="Yue Chinese">Yue Chinese</a> when the port city of <a href="/wiki/Guangzhou" title="Guangzhou">Guangzhou</a> on the <a href="/wiki/Pearl_River_Delta" title="Pearl River Delta">Pearl River Delta</a> became the largest port in China, with a trade network stretching as far as Arabia.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELi2006126_47-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELi2006126-47">[47]</a></sup> Cantonese was also used in the popular <i>Yu\xc3\xa8\'\xc5\x8du</i>, <i>M\xc3\xb9y\xc3\xba</i> and <i>N\xc3\xa1ny\xc4\xabn</i> folksong genres, as well as <a href="/wiki/Cantonese_opera" title="Cantonese opera">Cantonese opera</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEYue-Hashimoto19725.E2.80.936_48-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEYue-Hashimoto19725.E2.80.936-48">[48]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERamsey198799_49-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERamsey198799-49">[49]</a></sup> Additionally, a distinct classical literature was developed in Cantonese, with <a href="/wiki/Middle_Chinese" title="Middle Chinese">Middle Chinese</a> texts sounding more similar to modern Cantonese than other present-day Chinese varieties, including Mandarin.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEYue-Hashimoto19725_50-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEYue-Hashimoto19725-50">[50]</a></sup></p>\n<p>As Guangzhou became China\'s key commercial center for foreign trade and exchange in the 1700s, Cantonese became the variety of Chinese interacting with most with the Western World.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELi2006126_47-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELi2006126-47">[47]</a></sup> Around this period and continuing into the 1900s, the ancestors of most of the population of <a href="/wiki/Hong_Kong" title="Hong Kong">Hong Kong</a> and <a href="/wiki/Macau" title="Macau">Macau</a> arrived from Guangzhou and surrounding areas after they were ceded to <a href="/wiki/British_Empire" title="British Empire">Britain</a> and <a href="/wiki/Portuguese_Empire" title="Portuguese Empire">Portugal</a>, respectively.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEYue-Hashimoto197270_51-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEYue-Hashimoto197270-51">[51]</a></sup> A popular <a href="/wiki/Urban_legend" title="Urban legend">urban legend</a> claims that after the <a href="/wiki/Xinhai_Revolution" title="Xinhai Revolution">Xinhai Revolution</a> of 1912, Cantonese almost became the official language of the Republic of China but lost by just one vote.<sup id="cite_ref-52" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-52">[52]</a></sup></p>\n<p>In Mainland China, <a href="/wiki/Standard_Chinese" title="Standard Chinese">Standard Chinese</a> (based on <a href="/wiki/Mandarin_Chinese" title="Mandarin Chinese">Mandarin</a>) has been heavily promoted as the medium of instruction in schools and as the official language, especially after the communist takeover in 1949. Meanwhile, Cantonese has remained the official variety of Chinese in Hong Kong and Macau, both during and after the colonial period.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEZhangYang2004154_16-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEZhangYang2004154-16">[16]</a></sup></p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Cultural_role">Cultural role</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Cantonese&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: Cultural role">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div class="thumb tleft">\n<div class="thumbinner" style="width:152px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Guangzhouhua-vector.svg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d7/Guangzhouhua-vector.svg/150px-Guangzhouhua-vector.svg.png" width="150" height="219" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d7/Guangzhouhua-vector.svg/225px-Guangzhouhua-vector.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d7/Guangzhouhua-vector.svg/300px-Guangzhouhua-vector.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="249" data-file-height="364" /></a>\n<div class="thumbcaption">\n<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Guangzhouhua-vector.svg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>\n<i>Gw\xc3\xb3ngj\xc3\xa0u W\xc3\xa1h</i>, the historically common name for Standard Cantonese written in traditional (left) and simplified (right) Chinese characters</div>\n</div>\n</div>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/Cantonese_culture" class="mw-redirect" title="Cantonese culture">Cantonese culture</a></div>\n<p>Spoken Chinese has numerous regional and local varieties, many of which are <a href="/wiki/Mutually_unintelligible" class="mw-redirect" title="Mutually unintelligible">mutually unintelligible</a>. Most of these are rare outside their native areas, though they may be spoken outside of China. Since a 1909 <a href="/wiki/Qing_Dynasty" class="mw-redirect" title="Qing Dynasty">Qing Dynasty</a> decree, China has promoted <a href="/wiki/Standard_Chinese" title="Standard Chinese">Mandarin</a> for use in education, the media and official communications.<sup id="cite_ref-53" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-53">[53]</a></sup> The proclamation of Mandarin as the official national language however, was not fully accepted by the Cantonese authorities in the early 20th century, who argued for the "regional uniqueness" of their own local language and commercial importance of the region.<sup id="cite_ref-Cantonese_film_54-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Cantonese_film-54">[54]</a></sup> Unlike other non-Mandarin Chinese varieties, Cantonese persists in a few state <a href="/wiki/Southern_Television_Guangdong" class="mw-redirect" title="Southern Television Guangdong">television</a> and <a href="/wiki/Radio_Guangdong" title="Radio Guangdong">radio</a> broadcasts today.</p>\n<p>Nevertheless, there have been recent attempts to minimize the use of Cantonese in China. The most notable has been the 2010 proposal that <a href="/wiki/Guangzhou_Television" class="mw-redirect" title="Guangzhou Television">Guangzhou Television</a> increase its broadcast in Mandarin at the expense of Cantonese programs. This however led to <a href="/wiki/Guangzhou_Television_Cantonese_controversy" title="Guangzhou Television Cantonese controversy">protests in Guangzhou</a>, which eventually dissuaded authorities from going forward with the proposal.<sup id="cite_ref-55" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-55">[55]</a></sup> Additionally, there are reports of students being punished for speaking other Chinese languages at school, resulting in a reluctance of younger children to communicate in their native languages, including Cantonese.<sup id="cite_ref-ycwb10-07-09_56-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ycwb10-07-09-56">[56]</a></sup> Such actions have further provoked Cantonese speakers to cherish their linguistic identity in contrast to migrants who have generally arrived from poorer areas of China and largely speak Mandarin or other Chinese languages.<sup id="cite_ref-57" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-57">[57]</a></sup></p>\n<p>Due to the linguistic history of <a href="/wiki/Hong_Kong" title="Hong Kong">Hong Kong</a> and <a href="/wiki/Macau" title="Macau">Macau</a>, and the use of Cantonese in many established <a href="/wiki/Overseas_Chinese" title="Overseas Chinese">overseas Chinese</a> communities, diaspora speakers of Cantonese is numerous compared to speakers residing in China. Cantonese is the predominant Chinese variety spoken in Hong Kong and Macau. In these areas, public discourse takes place almost exclusively in Cantonese, making it the only variety of Chinese other than Mandarin to be used as an official language in the world. Because of their dominance in Chinese diaspora overseas, standard Cantonese and its dialect <a href="/wiki/Taishanese" title="Taishanese">Taishanese</a> are among the most common Chinese languages that one may encounter in the West.</p>\n<p>Increasingly since the <a href="/wiki/Handover_of_Hong_Kong" class="mw-redirect" title="Handover of Hong Kong">1997 Handover</a>, Cantonese has been used as a symbol of local identity in Hong Kong, largely through the <a href="/wiki/Democratic_development_in_Hong_Kong" title="Democratic development in Hong Kong">development of democracy in the territory</a> and <a href="/wiki/Desinicization" class="mw-redirect" title="Desinicization">desinicization</a> practices to emphasise a separate Hong Kong identity.<sup id="cite_ref-58" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-58">[58]</a></sup></p>\n<p>A similar identity issue exists in the United States, where conflicts have arisen for the Chinese speakers due to a large recent influx of Mandarin-speakers. While older Taiwanese immigrants have learned Cantonese to foster integration within the traditional Chinese American populations, more recent arrivals from the Mainland continue to use Mandarin exclusively. This has contributed to a segregation of communities based on linguistic cleavage. In particular, some Chinese Americans (including <a href="/wiki/American-born_Chinese" title="American-born Chinese">American-born Chinese</a>) of Cantonese background emphasise their non-Mainland origins(e.g. Hong Kong, Macau, Vietnam, etc.) to assert their identity in the face of new waves of immigration.<sup id="cite_ref-Chee_Beng_Tan_2007_115_38-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Chee_Beng_Tan_2007_115-38">[38]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-HML_41-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-HML-41">[41]</a></sup></p>\n<p>Along with Mandarin and <a href="/wiki/Hokkien" title="Hokkien">Hokkien</a>, Cantonese has its own popular music, <a href="/wiki/Cantopop" title="Cantopop">Cantopop</a>, which is the predominant genre in Hong Kong. Many artists from the Mainland and Taiwan have learned Cantonese to break into the market.<sup id="cite_ref-Donald_59-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Donald-59">[59]</a></sup> Popular native Mandarin-speaking singers, including <a href="/wiki/Faye_Wong" title="Faye Wong">Faye Wong</a>, <a href="/wiki/Eric_Moo" title="Eric Moo">Eric Moo</a>, and singers from Taiwan, have been trained in Cantonese to add "Hong Kong-ness" to their performances.<sup id="cite_ref-Donald_59-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Donald-59">[59]</a></sup></p>\n<p>Cantonese films date to the early days of Chinese cinema, and the first Cantonese talkie, <i>White Gold Dragon</i> (<a href="https://zh.wikipedia.org/wiki/%E7%99%BD%E9%87%91%E9%BE%99_(%E7%94%B5%E5%BD%B1)" class="extiw" title="zh:\xe7\x99\xbd\xe9\x87\x91\xe9\xbe\x99 (\xe7\x94\xb5\xe5\xbd\xb1)">\xe7\x99\xbd\xe9\x87\x91\xe9\xbe\x8d</a>), was made in 1932 by the <a href="/wiki/Tianyi_Film_Company" title="Tianyi Film Company">Tianyi Film Company</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-60" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-60">[60]</a></sup> Despite a ban on Cantonese films by the <a href="/wiki/Nanjing_decade" title="Nanjing decade">Nanjing authority</a> in the 1930s, Cantonese film production continued in Hong Kong which was then under British colonial rule.<sup id="cite_ref-Cantonese_film_54-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Cantonese_film-54">[54]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-61" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-61">[61]</a></sup> From the mid-1970s to the 1990s, <a href="/wiki/Cinema_of_Hong_Kong" title="Cinema of Hong Kong">Cantonese films</a> made in Hong Kong were very popular in the Chinese speaking world.</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Phonology">Phonology</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Cantonese&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: Phonology">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Cantonese_phonology" title="Cantonese phonology">Cantonese phonology</a></div>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/Hong_Kong_Cantonese" title="Hong Kong Cantonese">Hong Kong Cantonese</a></div>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Initials_and_finals">Initials and finals</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Cantonese&amp;action=edit&amp;section=23" title="Edit section: Initials and finals">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>The <i>de facto</i> standard pronunciation of Cantonese is that of Canton (Guangzhou), which is described in the Cantonese phonology article. <a href="/wiki/Hong_Kong_Cantonese" title="Hong Kong Cantonese">Hong Kong Cantonese</a> has some minor variations in phonology, but is largely identical to standard Guangzhou Cantonese.</p>\n<p>In Hong Kong and Macau, certain phoneme pairs have caused one sound to merge into another. Although termed as "lazy sound" (<span lang="zh-hk">\xe6\x87\xb6\xe9\x9f\xb3</span>) and considered substandard to Guangzhou pronunciation, the phenomenon has been widespread in the territories since the early 20th century. The most notable difference between Hong Kong and Guangzhou pronunciation is the substitution of the liquid nasal (<span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">/l/</span>) for the nasal initial (<span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">/n/</span>) in many words.<sup id="cite_ref-62" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-62">[62]</a></sup> An example of this is manifested in the word for you (\xe4\xbd\xa0), pronounced as <i>n\xc3\xa9ih</i> in Guangzhou and as <i>l\xc3\xa9ih</i> in Hong Kong.</p>\n<p>Another key feature of Hong Kong Cantonese is the merging of the two syllabic nasals <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">/\xc5\x8b\xcc\xa9/</span> and <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">/m\xcc\xa9/</span>. This can be exemplified in the elimination of the contrast of sounds between \xe5\x90\xb3 (<a href="/wiki/Ng_(surname)" class="mw-redirect" title="Ng (surname)">Ng</a>, a surname) (<i>ng</i><small>4</small>/<i>\xc7\xb9gh</i> in Guangzhou pronunciation) and \xe5\x94\x94 (not) (<i>mh</i><small>4</small>/m\xcc\x80h in Guangzhou pronunciation). In Hong Kong, both words are pronounced as the latter.<sup id="cite_ref-Matthews_63-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Matthews-63">[63]</a></sup></p>\n<p>Lastly, the initials <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">/k\xca\xb7/</span> and <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">/k\xca\xb7\xca\xb0/</span> can be merged into <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">/k/</span> and <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">/k\xca\xb0/</span> when followed by <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">/\xc9\x94\xcb\x90/</span>. An example is in the word for country (\xe5\x9c\x8b), pronounced in standard Guangzhou as <i>gwok</i> but as <i>gok</i> with the merge. Unlike the above two differences, this merge is found alongside the standard pronunciation in Hong Kong rather than being replaced. Educated speakers often stick to the standard pronunciation but can exemplify the merged pronunciation in casual speech. In contrast, less educated speakers pronounce the merge more frequently.<sup id="cite_ref-Matthews_63-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Matthews-63">[63]</a></sup></p>\n<p>Less prevalent, but still notable differences found among a number of Hong Kong speakers include:</p>\n<ul>\n<li>Merging of <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">/\xc5\x8b/</span> initial into null initial.</li>\n<li>Merging of <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">/\xc5\x8b/</span> and <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">/k/</span> codas into <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">/n/</span> and <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">/t/</span> codas respectively, eliminating contrast between these pairs of finals (except after <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">/e/</span> and <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">/o/</span>): <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">/a\xcb\x90n/</span>-<span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">/a\xcb\x90\xc5\x8b/</span>, <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">/a\xcb\x90t/</span>-<span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">/a\xcb\x90k/</span>, <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">/\xc9\x90n/</span>-<span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">/\xc9\x90\xc5\x8b/</span>, <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">/\xc9\x90t/</span>-<span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">/\xc9\x90k/</span>, <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">/\xc9\x94\xcb\x90n/</span>-<span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">/\xc9\x94\xcb\x90\xc5\x8b/</span> and <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">/\xc9\x94\xcb\x90t/</span>-<span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">/\xc9\x94\xcb\x90k/</span>.</li>\n<li>Merging of the rising tones (\xe9\x99\xb0\xe4\xb8\x8a 2nd and \xe9\x99\xbd\xe4\xb8\x8a 5th).<sup id="cite_ref-64" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-64">[64]</a></sup></li>\n</ul>\n<p>Cantonese vowels tend to be traced further back to <a href="/wiki/Middle_Chinese" title="Middle Chinese">Middle Chinese</a> back than their Mandarin analogues, such as M. /a\xc9\xaa/ vs. C. /\xc9\x94\xcb\x90i/; M. /i/ vs. C. /\xc9\x90i/; M. /\xc9\xa4/ vs. C. /\xc9\x94\xcb\x90/; M. /\xc9\x91\xca\x8a/ vs. C. /ou/ etc. For consonants, some differences include M. /\xc9\x95, t\xc9\x95, t\xc9\x95\xca\xb0/ vs. C. /h, k, k\xca\xb0/; M. /\xca\x90/ vs. C. /j/; and a greater syllable coda diversity in Cantonese (such as syllables ending in <i>-t</i>, <i>-p</i>, or <i>-k</i>).</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Tones">Tones</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Cantonese&amp;action=edit&amp;section=24" title="Edit section: Tones">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>Generally speaking, Cantonese is a <a href="/wiki/Tonal_language" class="mw-redirect" title="Tonal language">tonal language</a> with six phonetic tones.</p>\n<p>Historically, finals that end in a <a href="/wiki/Stop_consonant" title="Stop consonant">stop consonant</a> were considered as "<a href="/wiki/Checked_tone" title="Checked tone">checked tones</a>" and treated separately by <a href="/wiki/Historical_linguistics" title="Historical linguistics">diachronic</a> convention, identifying Cantonese with nine tones. However, phonetically these are now considered a conflation of tone and final consonant and are seldom counted as individual tones in modern linguistics.<sup id="cite_ref-65" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-65">[65]</a></sup></p>\n<table class="wikitable" style="text-align:center;">\n<tr>\n<th>Syllable type</th>\n<th colspan="6"></th>\n</tr>\n<tr>\n<th><a href="/wiki/Tone_name" title="Tone name">Tone name</a></th>\n<td>dark flat<br />\n(\xe9\x99\xb0\xe5\xb9\xb3)</td>\n<td>dark rising<br />\n(\xe9\x99\xb0\xe4\xb8\x8a)</td>\n<td>dark departing<br />\n(\xe9\x99\xb0\xe5\x8e\xbb)</td>\n<td>light flat<br />\n(\xe9\x99\xbd\xe5\xb9\xb3)</td>\n<td>light rising<br />\n(\xe9\x99\xbd\xe4\xb8\x8a)</td>\n<td>light departing<br />\n(\xe9\x99\xbd\xe5\x8e\xbb)</td>\n</tr>\n<tr>\n<th>Description</th>\n<td>high level,<br />\nhigh falling</td>\n<td>medium rising</td>\n<td>medium level</td>\n<td>low falling,<br />\nvery low level</td>\n<td>low rising</td>\n<td>low level</td>\n</tr>\n<tr>\n<th><a href="/wiki/Yale_romanization_of_Cantonese" title="Yale romanization of Cantonese">Yale</a> or <a href="/wiki/Jyutping" title="Jyutping">Jyutping</a><br />\n<a href="/wiki/Tone_number" title="Tone number">tone number</a></th>\n<td>1</td>\n<td>2</td>\n<td>3</td>\n<td>4</td>\n<td>5</td>\n<td>6</td>\n</tr>\n<tr>\n<th>Example</th>\n<td>\xe8\xa9\xa9</td>\n<td>\xe5\x8f\xb2</td>\n<td>\xe8\xa9\xa6</td>\n<td>\xe6\x99\x82</td>\n<td>\xe5\xb8\x82</td>\n<td>\xe6\x98\xaf</td>\n</tr>\n<tr>\n<th><a href="/wiki/Tone_letter" title="Tone letter">Tone letter</a></th>\n<td><span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">si\xcb\x90\xcb\xa5</span>, <span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">si\xcb\x90\xcb\xa5\xcb\xa7</span></td>\n<td><span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">si\xcb\x90\xcb\xa7\xcb\xa5</span></td>\n<td><span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">si\xcb\x90\xcb\xa7</span></td>\n<td><span class="IPA">si\xcb\x90\xcb\xa9</span></td>\n<td><span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA">si\xcb\x90\xcb\xa9\xcb\xa7</span></td>\n<td><span
